Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)
♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ polytheistic ♥
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ uncertain_present_absent ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_346_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "Greeks did not necessarily assume that the gods could read their minds and knew the inner secrets of their heart and so forth. However, they definitely assumed that gods could see and hear everything they did. I don’t think the gods were concerned with people’s thoughts unless these thoughts manifested themselves in words or actions. This is not to say that they could never read minds, just that it wasn’t part and parcel of how they were represented, the way it is, for example, with the Christian god.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_347_AfTochr] In Hinduism: "Each Purana tends to focus on a specific deity as the Supreme Being responsible for creating and ordering the world. [...] Thus, [for example,] Krishna [...] is a form of the supreme God who creates, maintains, and destroys the universe. In the Bhagavad Gita , Arjuna is permitted to glimpse Krishna’s cosmic form. He sees the entire universe, the sun, stars, and planets, and all the people gathered on the battlefield within the body of Krishna. Krishna tells him that everything that exists is part of himself, but that he is not limited to everything that exists. In other words, just as the Vedic Purusha extended ten finger-lengths beyond the created universe, so too Krishna is the entire cosmos and more." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_348_AfTochr] In Zoroastrianism: "The name of the supreme God of the Zoroastrian faith is Ahura Mazda (or Ohrmazd). He is self-created, omniscient, omnipresent, holy, invisible, and beyond human conceptualization (Yasna 4.7, 29.4, 31.13, 45.5; Yasht 1.7, 1.8, 1.12, 1.13-14; Videvdat 2.1, 19.20)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_349_AfTochr] In Buddhism: "There are numerous Higher Gods, but these are remote and sublime beings who have little involvement in human affairs. Nevertheless, they are subject to karma and are eventually reborn like everyone else." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_350_AfTochr]
♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ uncertain_present_absent ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_351_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not enough appears to be known about the latter to be able to say whether the gods they focussed on were thought to be omniscient. And, similarly, it is not very clear whether omniscience would have been seen as a characteristic of Siva. However, even by looking only at the remaining three religions, it is clear that this variable cannot be coded as either "absent" or "present". Regarding Buddhism, the idea of an all-knowing God runs contrary to the emphasis that this religion places on an individual's responsibility for their own spiritual development [MAJIDBENAM_REF_352_AfTochr]. Regarding Zoroastrianism, the god Ahura-Mazda is a fairly straightforward omniscient deity [MAJIDBENAM_REF_353_AfTochr]. And as for Greek deities, “Everything depends on what you mean by “omniscience.” First, there is the distinction between “knowing everything important,” like who committed a crime or who will win the war, and true omniscience, which includes everything which can be known (things like “the fall of a sparrow” and how many grains of sand are on a beach). Very few Greek gods claimed true omniscience. The only example I know is that it was claimed for Apollo at Delphi, who was specifically an oracular god. So it was his business to know everything. The claim seems to have been put forward by the sanctuary in order to enhance his prestige. Greek philosophers presumably believed in divine omniscience because they represented the gods or the divine as perfect, which would require absolute knowledge, but these issues do not seem to have really come to the fore in the literature until the Roman period and Christianity, so far as I can tell. Even if omniscience only means “knowing everything that it is important to know,” I have my doubts about whether the Greek gods possessed it. I would say that it was always contextual. They knew whatever it was important to know in a given context. Greeks did not necessarily assume that the gods could read their minds and knew the inner secrets of their heart and so forth. However, they definitely assumed that gods could see and hear everything they did. I don’t think the gods were concerned with people’s thoughts unless these thoughts manifested themselves in words or actions. This is not to say that they could never read minds, just that it wasn’t part and parcel of how they were represented, the way it is, for example, with the Christian god.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_354_AfTochr]
♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ uncertain_present_absent ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_355_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: In Zoroastrianism: "In taking the immense step of seeing Ahura Mazda as God, rather than only as one of the great gods of the Iranian pantheon, Zoroaster stopped short of conceiving him as omnipotent. According to his revelation, suffering and evil in this world, and torment in the next, have a source other than Ahura Mazda. He can diminish and in the end annihilate them, but not control or direct them in the present."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_356_AfTochr] In Buddhism: "There are numerous Higher Gods, but these are remote and sublime beings who have little involvement in human affairs. Nevertheless, they are subject to karma and are eventually reborn like everyone else." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_357_AfTochr]
♠ High Gods (creator gods) ♣ [moralizing; inactive] ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_358_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: In Hinduism: "Among the cosmic laws that constitute dharma, the law of karma, or action, is especially relevant to rebirth. By the law of karma, every thought, word, and deed carries with it a like reaction. Beings are born, live, die, and are reborn based on their actions. Every good deed brings a reward, and every bad deed a punishment. The terms ‘reward’ and ‘punishment’ are deceptive, however; for karma is not seen as a set of rewards and punishments doled out by a just God, but in impersonal terms, as the working of natural law. Karma attracts reactions to moral agents like an ethical law of gravity. Benevolent actions produce good results for the person who engages in them. Similarly, actions intended to cause suffering will lead to suffering for those who commit them." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_359_AfTochr] In Zoroastrianism, the following quote makes it clear the supreme god, Ahura Mazda, was both responsible for the creation of the world and for punishing transgressors. "[T]he prophet feels also the awe due to the God who is “Creator of all” (Y. 44.7), and who, as Lord of aša, presides over a strict justice for mankind by which the wicked, who have chosen to act “at Lie’s commands” (Y. 31.1) will be in due course condemned. Their destination is the Worst Existence, i.e., Hell, which has been brought into being by Angra Mainyu (Y. 30.4). Mazdā has “appointed recompenses” (Y. 43.5), decreeing that “the end shall be different for each” (Y. 48.4); but he “has left to men’s wills (the choice between) holy and unholy” (Y. 45.9), and it is their own inner selves (daēnā-) which lead sinners to destruction (Y. 51.13)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_360_AfTochr] In Buddhism: "There are numerous Higher Gods, but these are remote and sublime beings who have little involvement in human affairs. Nevertheless, they are subject to karma and are eventually reborn like everyone else." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_361_AfTochr][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Supernatural Enforcement of Morality
♠ supernatural enforcement against harm ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_362_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "The most important traditional religious teaching on this subject was that the gods required people to treat “strangers and suppliants” well. That is, you should assist strangers who come to your door in need (and definitely not harm them) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_363_AfTochr] In Hinduism: "Coming from the Sanskrit root word Dhar (sustain), Dharma has been interpreted variously as a religious code of behavior, a divine system of morality and righteousness, and conformation to one’s cosmically allotted duty and nature (Radhakrishnan, 1968). It is a comprehensive term, incorporating benevolence to humanity, moral restraint (yama), and observance of duty or obligation (niyama). Yama required the active avoidance of violence (ahimsa), dishonesty, falsehood, jealousy, avarice, and other vices." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_364_AfTochr] In Zoroastrianism: According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include homicide, "neglecting crying and hungry children" and "denying hospitality to travellers"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_365_AfTochr]. In Buddhism: "The practice of good conduct takes the form of commitment to the five precepts usually recited in conjunction with the three refuges: (1) to refrain from harming living creatures"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_366_AfTochr] "Essentially the psychological states that motivate the ten unwholesome courses of action - strong greed, hatred, and delusion- lead to rebirth in the unhappy destinies or ‘descents’: in a hell realm, as a hungry ghost, an animal, or a jealous god. In fact rather a precise correlation exists here: dominated by greed one becomes a hungry ghost, a class of beings ever discontent and anguished because of being unable to satisfy their greed; dominated by hatred one enters one of the hell realms where one suffers terrible pain; dominated by ignorance one becomes an animal ruled by the instincts of food and reproduction." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_367_AfTochr]
♠ supernatural enforcement of fairness ♣ uncertain_present_absent ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_368_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: Food was distributed during certain sacrificial ceremonies, but it was done to gain prestige rather than for any reward from the gods[MAJIDBENAM_REF_369_AfTochr]. In Hinduism: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_370_AfTochr] In Zoroastrianism: "The easiest way to avoid hell, of course, is to accumulate more good thoughts, words or deeds than bad. Some virtues, however, are praised as particularly efficient to avoid hell.20 A catechism highlights gratefulness (ČHP 30 [= Kanga 1960:16-17]). This virtue is also praised in the wisdom literature as a way to save one’s soul (Dk. VI 120; E38c; E45f [= Shaked 1979:48-49; 206-7; 214-15])"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_371_AfTochr] In Buddhism: "In the cosmology set out above, karma functions as the elevator that takes people from one floor of the building to another. Good deeds result in an upward movement and bad deeds in a downward one. Karma is not a system of rewards and punishments meted out by God but a kind of natural law akin to the law of gravity. [...] Buddhists speak of good karma as ‘merit’ (punna, Sanskrit: punya), and much effort is expended in acquiring it. Some picture it as a kind of spiritual capital - like money in a bank account - whereby credit is built up as the deposit on a heavenly rebirth. One of the best ways for a layman to earn merit is by supporting the order of monks. This can be done by placing food in the bowls of monks as they pass on their daily alms round, by providing robes for the monks, by listening to sermons and attending religious services, and by donating funds for the upkeep of monasteries and temples." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_372_AfTochr] "The nature of bad action is usually illustrated by reference to a list of the ten courses of unwholesome action (karma/kamma-patha)which consist of three bodily courses of action (taking life, taking what is not given, sexual misconduct), four vocal courses of action (lying, divisive speech, hurtful speech, frivolous speech), and three mental courses of action (covetousness, ill-will, wrong view). [...] Essentially the psychological states that motivate the ten unwholesome courses of action - strong greed, hatred, and delusion - lead to rebirth in the unhappy destinies or ‘descents’: in a hell realm, as a hungry ghost, an animal, or a jealous god. In fact rather a precise correlation exists here: dominated by greed one becomes a hungry ghost, a class of beings ever discontent and anguished because of being unable to satisfy their greed; dominated by hatred one enters one of the hell realms where one suffers terrible pain; dominated by ignorance one becomes an animal ruled by the instincts of food and reproduction." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_373_AfTochr]
♠ supernatural enforcement of human reciprocity ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_374_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "It is now evident that, for Hesiod, κακότης‎ represents moral badness and the opposite to virtue, arete. A bad man (κακὸς ἀνήρ‎) does not follow justice (δίκη‎) with his mind, and acts immorally; he represents a source of religious pollution and danger for his community. Just as Hesiod connects the bad man with injustice and religious danger, so he presents moral righteousness and adherence to justice (δίκη‎) as coexisting with ritual correctness. Gregory Nagy points out that in Op. 336-41, a passage which follows the lines quoted above, the moral correctness of δίκη‎ is ‘programmatically re-enforced by the ritual correctness of the just man’:26 Perses is again reminded that gods punish unjust deeds (ἄδικα ἔργα‎; vv. 325-34), and that he should often sacrifice purely and cleanly to the deathless gods and propitiate them often with libations and incense (vv. 336-41). Religious correctness is thus laid out as avoiding unjust deeds and as honouring the gods with sacrifices and libations.//"In vv. 325-34 the following deeds are listed as unjust (ἄδικα ἔργα‎): gaining wealth by force, stealing, or deception; wronging a suppliant or a guest; engaging in illicit sexual intercourse; offending orphans or aged parents. These offences span both the social and religious spheres. This is stressed at the end of the passage, where Hesiod states that such deeds provoke the anger of Zeus himself, who inflicts grave punishments (vv. 333-4)"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_375_AfTochr] Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_376_AfTochr] In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_377_AfTochr] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include "violation of contracts", "selling items with false measures and weights" and "making false promises"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_378_AfTochr] In Buddhism: "The nature of bad action is usually illustrated by reference to a list of the ten courses of unwholesome action (karma/kamma-patha)which consist of three bodily courses of action (taking life, taking what is not given, sexual misconduct), four vocal courses of action (lying, divisive speech, hurtful speech, frivolous speech), and three mental courses of action (covetousness, ill-will, wrong view). [...] Essentially the psychological states that motivate the ten unwholesome courses of action - strong greed, hatred, and delusion - lead to rebirth in the unhappy destinies or ‘descents’: in a hell realm, as a hungry ghost, an animal, or a jealous god. In fact rather a precise correlation exists here: dominated by greed one becomes a hungry ghost, a class of beings ever discontent and anguished because of being unable to satisfy their greed; dominated by hatred one enters one of the hell realms where one suffers terrible pain; dominated by ignorance one becomes an animal ruled by the instincts of food and reproduction." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_379_AfTochr]
♠ supernatural enforcement of property rights ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_380_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_382_AfTochr] "Universal dharma relates to all humanity and it includes ethical virtues such as compassion, generosity, gratitude, and non-violence. These virtues that are said to be common to all humans are known as sadharana (common) or sanatana (eternal) dharma. For example, the Law of Manu (X:63) argues that non-injury, truth, not stealing, purity and control of the senses summarise dharma for the four castes." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_383_AfTochr] In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). In Buddhism: "The heart of Buddhist spiritual life, whether lay or monastic, is punya (Pali punna ). This term is usually translated as ‘merit’ and refers to certain significant kinds of action that are regarded as auspicious and potent deeds. Essentially, merit is good ‘karma’ (karman/kamma) : wholesome (kusla/kusala)deeds which plant seeds that bear fortunate and pleasant fruits, whether in this life or lives to come, and thereby create conditions for further meritorious action in the future. These are the kinds of action that conduce to the development of the qualities of the eightfold path. Moreover it is making merit that binds the lay and monastic communities together."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_386_AfTochr] "The nature of bad action is usually illustrated by reference to a list of the ten courses of unwholesome action" which include "taking what is not given" and "covetousness" [...] "Essentially the psychological states that motivate the ten unwholesome courses of action - strong greed, hatred, and delusion - lead to rebirth in the unhappy destinies or ‘descents’: in a hell realm, as a hungry ghost, an animal, or a jealous god. In fact rather a precise correlation exists here: dominated by greed one becomes a hungry ghost, a class of beings ever discontent and anguished because of being unable to satisfy their greed; dominated by hatred one enters one of the hell realms where one suffers terrible pain; dominated by ignorance one becomes an animal ruled by the instincts of food and reproduction." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_387_AfTochr]
♠ supernatural enforcement of kinship obligations ♣ suspected unknown ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_388_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "Although Homer's epic depicts a few exceptional figures like Tantalos and Sisyphos receiving punishment in the underworld (Od. 11 576-600), elaborate descriptions of underworld retributions come mostly from later sources, causing some scholars to suppose that a compensatory afterlife is a later invention. However, not only does such compensation appear in our earliest textual source, but Pausanias (10.28.4-6) tells us of the great painting of the underworld by the fifth-century BCE painter Polygnotos, which included such depictions. [...] A man who treated his father unjustly is being throttled by him, while another who plundered a temple is being tormented by a female skilled in poisons. Pausanias comments that the Greeks in Polygnotos' time thought that failing to respect parents and the gods were the worst crimes, and that is why Polygnotos has illustrated these cases. Polygnotos' painting, while exceptional in size and scope, is not exceptional in its subject matter: numerous vase paintings show scenes of underworld reward or punishment, and a Demosthenic speech mentions paintings depicting the afterlife torments of the impious as a familiar trope for his audience ([Dem.] 25.53)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_389_AfTochr] In Hinduism: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_390_AfTochr] "As a religiously informed orientation to life’s actions and obligations, dharma gives guidelines to how husbands, wives, children, and parents should conduct themselves. Many of the selections from sources included here advise their audiences on the roles proper for various situations and consider some of the complexities and ambiguities that arise in living according to their discipline. [...] Deeply embedded in Hindu notions of marriage is a disposition toward protection. It is the husband’s obligation to protect his wife from external predators and temptations and to invest in a relationship that will sustain her commitment to him and his world. At the same time, it is the obligation of the wife to protect her husband by feeding, caring for him, and investing in a relationship that will sustain his commitment." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_391_AfTochr] In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_392_AfTochr] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include "disrespect of husband" and "disrespect for one's parents"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_393_AfTochr]. In Buddhism: "The heart of Buddhist spiritual life, whether lay or monastic, is punya (Pali punna ). This term is usually translated as ‘merit’ and refers to certain significant kinds of action that are regarded as auspicious and potent deeds. Essentially, merit is good ‘karma’ (karman/kamma) : wholesome (kusla/kusala)deeds which plant seeds that bear fortunate and pleasant fruits, whether in this life or lives to come, and thereby create conditions for further meritorious action in the future."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_394_AfTochr] "‘Right action’ basically means to act gently and kindly and to be ‘compassionate to all living beings’. What ‘right action’ and ‘right livelihood’ positively involve becomes clearer in the numerous practical guidelines for dealing with one’s parents, teachers, spouse, children, friends, one’s master and servant, Brahmans and Sramanas. So, for example, in the very popular Sigalovada Sutta (DN 31): Children should honour their parents, be grateful and support them when they are in need or grow old; parents should show their love to their children by teaching them virtue, training them for a profession and passing on their inheritance when it is due; [...] a husband should minister to his wife ‘by honouring her, by not disparaging her, by not being unfaithful to her, by giving authority to her, by providing her with adornments’. A wife will reciprocate ‘by properly organising her work, by being kind to the servants, by not being unfaithful, by protecting stores, and by being skilful and diligent in all she has to do’." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_395_AfTochr]
♠ supernatural enforcement of in-group loyalty ♣ inferred absent ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_396_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_397_AfTochr] Staufield's comprehensive list of all the sins mentioned in the Arda Viraz does not include anything that might be linked to in-group loyalty. In Buddhism: "As mentioned before, the development of a loving mind towards all beings, whether friend or foe, is the aim of one of the meditative practices of early Buddhism (see above p. 53f ). In his instructions for this kind of meditation, Buddhaghosa recommends starting with a wish for one’s own happiness: ‘May I be happy and free from suffering.’ Only if one is able to love oneself will one be capable of extending the same love to others. And so Buddhaghosa then suggests directing this loving mental attitude to a dear friend, then to a neutral person and finally towards a hostile person until it becomes possible to bear love towards all beings indiscriminately, such that one sincerely wishes that ‘all may be happy and free from suffering’ (see Vism 9:1- 12). Buddhaghosa is realistic enough to expect some difficulties: If someone is unable to overcome ill feelings against a particular person, one should imagine that in a former reincarnation the person in question has been one’s own beloved mother, father, brother, sister, son, daughter or friend. For, as Buddhaghosa quotes the canonical scriptures, within the unfathomable length of saņsaric existence ‘it is not easy to find a being who has not formerly been your mother … your father …’ (SN 15:14- 19), etc. (see Vism 9:36; Nanamoli, 1999, p. 297f ). Making oneself aware of the universal kinship of all beings should aid in becoming free of negative feelings. The crucial importance of overcoming every form of hatred or ill will was emphasised by the Buddha himself in his parable of the saw, which Buddhaghosa recalls: Even if bandits would cut one literally to pieces with a sharp saw, one should not develop any hatred or hostility against them, but encompass them with loving-kindness (see MN 21)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_398_AfTochr] "[...] Buddhism maintained a fairly flexible posture vis-a-vis competing religions and never sought to require laity to identify themselves exclusively as Buddhist or to rely singularly on the Buddhist clergy for all their spiritual needs." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_399_AfTochr]
♠ supernatural enforcement of respect ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_400_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: In Hinduism: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_401_AfTochr] "The Brahmins, although ‘priests’, were higher in the caste hierarchy than the Kshatriyas, the ‘rulers’. Not all Brahmins have been priests and not all Kshatriyas have been rulers. The complexity of the caste system, regional and linguistic differences and historical change have all militated against this rigidity. [...]Brahmins have traditionally been seen as ritually more pure than Kshatriyas and more versed in religious knowledge. They are, in theory if not always in fact, the key scholars in the Hindu tradition. The Kshatriyas have traditionally been seen, again in theory if not always in practice, as the rulers who maintain society, uphold dharma and protect the householder system. They built and sponsored temples, protected their subjects and their deities and, to that extent, the Brahmins recognised a ruler’s overlordship. However, the kingly role in ruling and guardianship duties involved more ‘polluting tendencies’ than were usual in a Brahmin’s life. The Brahmins have had a dual role in society. Those who are active priests in temples have engaged in functions that involve being given gifts by worshippers and such temple priests are more involved with lower castes. Paradoxically Brahmins who are versed in promoting scholarship and in advancing religious learning but who do not act as serving priests are, in general, more widely admired because they have less contact with the public. Thus Brahmins who are not priests can be more akin to those renouncers engaged in lifelong learning and spirituality, rather than in the hurly-burly of householder life. To that extent they would receive greater respect than Kshatriyas." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_402_AfTochr] In Zoroastrianism: "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Arda Viraz Namag (The Book of the Righteous Viraz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians. The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_403_AfTochr] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include "disrespect of husband"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_404_AfTochr]. In Buddhism: "In the cosmology set out above, karma functions as the elevator that takes people from one floor of the building to another. Good deeds result in an upward movement and bad deeds in a downward one. Karma is not a system of rewards and punishments meted out by God but a kind of natural law akin to the law of gravity. [...] Buddhists speak of good karma as ‘merit’ (punna, Sanskrit: punya), and much effort is expended in acquiring it. Some picture it as a kind of spiritual capital - like money in a bank account - whereby credit is built up as the deposit on a heavenly rebirth. One of the best ways for a layman to earn merit is by supporting the order of monks. This can be done by placing food in the bowls of monks as they pass on their daily alms round, by providing robes for the monks, by listening to sermons and attending religious services, and by donating funds for the upkeep of monasteries and temples." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_405_AfTochr]

In Buddhism: In Buddhism: "In marked contrast to the view of Islam about the fate of warriors who die in a holy war, the Buddha expresses the view in the Sutta Nipata (Sn.iv.308-11) that soldiers who die in battle go not to a special paradise but to a special hell, since at the moment of death their minds were bent on killing. Some texts affirm that even killing in self-defence, or in defence of family or friends, is wrongful, and in general an attitude of non-resistance in the face of violence is commended. The commentary to the Dhammapada relates how on one occasion when the Buddha's kinsmen, the Sakyas, were under attack, they allowed themselves to be slaughtered rather than break the First Precept. Other examples in the Jatakas and elsewhere speak of princes and kings who renounced their thrones rather than resorting to violence to defend their kingdoms." ♠ supernatural enforcement of purity ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_411_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "Hesiod is generally quick to remark that the gods are pleased when the rites are performed correctly, and are displeased when proper ritual purity is not obtained. [...] We can also observe different grades of divine anger: libating with unwashed hands renders the ritual null and void, and the gods refuse to communicate with the worshipper. The failure to achieve the required state of purity results in the failure of the ritual. A graver and more serious offence is committed when someone crosses the river without prayer and the ritual washing—then the gods are not only irate, but also keen to punish. This makes sense: the rivers are divinities, and by entering them humans establish a particularly close contact with the divine. It is one of the basic premises of Greek religion that gods abhor pollution, which is why ritual cleansing is a necessary prerequisite for every ritual action. This would imply that entering a river requires a particularly stringent adherence to ritual purity, while avoiding this purification inspires a particularly harsh punishment—a punishment for jeopardizing the god’s own purity. The intensity of the contact with the divinity corresponds directly to the intensity of the purity required for the worshipper and to the severity of the divine punishment if this requirement is violated."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_412_AfTochr] In Hinduism: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths...So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_413_AfTochr] "The strength of the caste system has been enhanced by another principle emphasised by Dumont (1980) in his classical work entitled Homo Hierarchicus: The Caste System and its Implications. According to Dumont, in addition to the distinctions already outlined that fit varnas and jatis into hierarchies, another must be added. This distinction takes caste hierarchical divides beyond the kind of class distinctions found in eighteenth-century Britain or in the Ancien Regime in Europe, by stressing the opposition between purity and pollution. Caste difference, according to this classification, goes beyond class differences into this other realm of ritual purity and pollution. The Brahmins are the highest and purest varna, and the Dalits are the lowest, in that they are outside varna and are impure altogether. The other varnas rank in between. Pollution occurs in different ways. It occurs through bodily contact of one sort or another, with menstrual cycles, through birth and death, through emissions such as faeces, urine and saliva, and through contact with night-soil, dirty clothes, unswept rooms and so on. Thus marriage and sex have to function within the correct set of jatis in the correct varna. Polluting jobs such as laundering and sweeping are done by people of lower castes so that the blood of people of higher castes can remain ‘pure’. Equally menstrual activity among older women makes them more ‘impure’ than men or pre-menstrual virgins, and therefore women are more polluting than men. They will thus often live semi-separately while menstruating, during which time they will also avoid going to the temple and therefore ‘polluting’ the gods. Furthermore, untouchable Dalits were prohibited from entering many temples before the post-Independence freedom-of-temple-entry legislation and even now they are kept out of some temples in many villages. [...] It is also true to say that Dumont’s thesis suffers from exaggeration and other matters are important in discussing caste. Nevertheless, there is at least an element of truth in the notion that purity and pollution has relevance in any discussion of caste." The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_415_AfTochr] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include "sodomy", "sexual intercourse during menstruation" and "polluting water and fire through excrement and carrion"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_416_AfTochr]. ♠ general supernatural enforcement of morality ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_418_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. In Greek religion: "Although Homer's epic depicts a few exceptional figures like Tantalos and Sisyphos receiving punishment in the underworld (Od. 11 576-600), elaborate descriptions of underworld retributions come mostly from later sources, causing some scholars to suppose that a compensatory afterlife is a later invention. However, not only does such compensation appear in our earliest textual source, but Pausanias (10.28.4-6) tells us of the great painting of the underworld by the fifth-century BCE painter Polygnotos, which included such depictions. [...] A man who treated his father unjustly is being throttled by him, while another who plundered a temple is being tormented by a female skilled in poisons. Pausanias comments that the Greeks in Polygnotos' time thought that failing to respect parents and the gods were the worst crimes, and that is why Polygnotos has illustrated these cases. Polygnotos' painting, while exceptional in size and scope, is not exceptional in its subject matter: numerous vase paintings show scenes of underworld reward or punishment, and a Demosthenic speech mentions paintings depicting the afterlife torments of the impious as a familiar trope for his audience ([Dem.] 25.53)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_419_AfTochr] In Hinduism: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_420_AfTochr] "Coming from the Sanskrit root word Dhar (sustain), Dharma has been interpreted variously as a religious code of behavior, a divine system of morality and righteousness, and conformation to one’s cosmically allotted duty and nature (Radhakrishnan, 1968). It is a comprehensive term, incorporating benevolence to humanity, moral restraint (yama), and observance of duty or obligation (niyama). Yama required the active avoidance of violence (ahimsa), dishonesty, falsehood, jealousy, avarice, and other vices." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_421_AfTochr] Hinduism: Historical Setting. In Palmer, M.D. and S.M. Burgess (eds) Wiley-Blackwell Companion to Religion and Social Justice pp. 110-123. Hoboken: John Wiley and Sons.</ref> In Zoroastrianism: "The easiest way to avoid hell, of course, is to accumulate more good thoughts, words or deeds than bad. Some virtues, however, are praised as particularly efficient to avoid hell.20 A catechism highlights gratefulness (ČHP 30 [= Kanga 1960:16-17]). This virtue is also praised in the wisdom literature as a way to save one’s soul (Dk. VI 120; E38c; E45f [= Shaked 1979:48-49; 206-7; 214-15]), sometimes in conjunction with other virtues such as contentment and tenderness. Generosity is also emphasized as saving the soul from hell (Dk. IX 6.3 [see West 1892:179]), as is righteousness (Dk. IX 17.3 [see West 1892:204])."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_422_AfTochr]

In Greek religion: "Although Homer's epic depicts a few exceptional figures like Tantalos and Sisyphos receiving punishment in the underworld (Od. 11 576-600), elaborate descriptions of underworld retributions come mostly from later sources, causing some scholars to suppose that a compensatory afterlife is a later invention. [...] A man who treated his father unjustly is being throttled by him, while another who plundered a temple is being tormented by a female skilled in poisons. VI D1b [= Shaked 1979:176-77]), standing when urinating (MX 1.39 [Skjærvø 2005:242]), or ignorance, bad knowledge and lack of wisdom (WZ 30.38-39 [= Gignoux and Tafazzoli 1993:104-7])."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_425_AfTochr] In Buddhism: "The heart of Buddhist spiritual life, whether lay or monastic, is punya (Pali punna ). This term is usually translated as ‘merit’ and refers to certain significant kinds of action that are regarded as auspicious and potent deeds. Essentially, merit is good ‘karma’ (karman/kamma) : wholesome (kusla/kusala)deeds which plant seeds that bear fortunate and pleasant fruits, whether in this life or lives to come, and thereby create conditions for further meritorious action in the future. These are the kinds of action that conduce to the development of the qualities of the eightfold path. Moreover it is making merit that binds the lay and monastic communities together. The traditional ‘bases of merit’ are generosity (dana), ethical conduct (sila/sila ), and meditation (bhavana ). [...]The practice of good conduct takes the form of commitment to the five precepts usually recited in conjunction with the three refuges: (1) to refrain from harming living creatures, (2) to refrain from taking what is not given, (3) to refrain from sexual misconduct, (4) to refrain from false speech, (5) to refrain from intoxicants that cause heedlessness (see pp. 170- 2). In addition the lay disciple may on special occasions (traditionally the full moon ‘observance’ days) or for longer periods take the eight precepts, replacing the third precept (refraining from sexual misconduct) with complete sexual abstinence and adding (6) refraining from eating after midday, (7) refraining from attending entertainments and using perfumes, etc., and (8) refraining from using luxurious beds."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_426_AfTochr]
♠ other supernatural enforcement not related to piety ♣ present ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_427_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. In myth, Tantalos and Lykaion are both punished for offering human flesh to the gods--which is not only a form of impiety, but also "broke the alimentary which helped articulate the cosmos; it thus endangered the cosmic order, threatening to drag the gods, who are its guarantors, to the level of bestiality to which Tantalos himself had drifted, to make the very guarantors of the cosmic order transgress one of the fundamental rules which help articulate the universe. Tantalos' transgression of the taboo against cannibalism also endangered human society, for it threatened the breakdown of civilization and a reversion to savagery, of which cannibalism was perceived to be an important characteristic."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_428_AfTochr] In Zoroastrianism: "Likewise there are some sins that immediately lead to hell, such as performing worship while thinking that the gods do not exist (Dk. ♠ in-group loyalty ♣ suspected unknown ♥ At different times during Kushan history, and in different combinations, Kushan emperors patronised Greek religion, Hinduism (specifically, Siva worship), Zoroastrianism, and Buddhism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_441_AfTochr], as well as a number of less well-known Indo-Iranian cults. Not much is known about the latter. Greek religion/ideology: a number of laws, traditions and regulations were linked to the protection of moral values[MAJIDBENAM_REF_442_AfTochr], but many of these do not appear to be sufficiently religion-based for our interests. Hinduism: main expectation appears to have been of supernatural, not human, enforcement, specifically through reincarnation. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_443_AfTochr] Zoroastrianism: in Sasanian Zoroastrianism, "all offenses against religious norms which could endanger the soul of the delinquent [...] were subject to punishment by religious authorities." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_444_AfTochr] Buddhism: main expectation appears to have been of supernatural, not human, enforcement, specifically karma.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_445_AfTochr] Overall, it is unclear how all these different systems interacted in this polity, and how either the elites or the commoners in this polity interpreted these (often foreign) systems.

State Intervention in Economy

♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ ♥
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ inferred absent ♥ Several different foreign and indigenous coins dating to this period have been found in Bactria and Transoxania, but the conquering Yuezhi seem unlikely to have fixed the value of the coinage. At Tillya Tepe, 'two different types of coins [were found] in a single grave, with at least a century between them'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_511_AfTochr] Schiltz comments that for nomads, the 'prestige' of coins 'weighed more heavily than monetary value'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_512_AfTochr]
♠ Public markets ♣ present ♥ Chinese historian Sima Qian wrote that 'After the Great Yüeh-chih moved west and attacked and conquered Ta-hsia [Bactria], the entire country came under their sway [...] The capital is called the city of Lan-shih [Bactra] and has a market where all sorts of goods are bought and sold'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_513_AfTochr][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Contracts

Public Goods[edit]

♠ Famine relief ♣ ♥ 'There is ... insufficient material to throw light on the history of the cities and inhabited rural localities in the region during this "dark age". No doubt a number of cities such as Ay Khanum perished in the course of the nomadic conquest. But it is equally clear that the nomads did not make a special effort to wreck the irrigation systems and the towns and villages. So far as we can judge, the nomads sought to exploit rather than destroy the existing economic structure.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_514_AfTochr] For the Yuezhi, this period seems to have been one of transition from a nomadic to a settled lifestyle: 'At first contemptuous of city life and Greek culture, the nomads gradually became enamored of the seductive Hellenic tradition. They rebuilt the cities they had sacked and created the great Kushan Empire on their own debris'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_515_AfTochr] Unfortunately the details of this transformation remain murky: 'In the time between the Greco-Bactrian period and the flowering of the Kushans, ancient records fail us'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_516_AfTochr]

But it is equally clear that the nomads did not make a special effort to wreck the irrigation systems and the towns and villages. ♠ provision of alimentary supplementation ♣ ♥
♠ Drinking water ♣ uncertain_present_absent ♥ The Graeco-Bactrian city of Aï Khanum had public fountains.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_523_AfTochr] However, Aï Khanum was sacked in nomadic invasions, and it is not clear whether drinking water provision would have continued in other areas under the Yuezhi.
♠ provision of drinking water ♣ ♥
♠ Irrigation works ♣ present ♥ Irrigation works predate the arrival of Yuezhi and Scythian nomads in Bactria: 'A very important point, deduced mainly from nomadic graves in Bactria, is that the graves are situated on the edge of the valleys and do not encroach on the irrigated and tilled areas. The very plausible suggestion has been made that the nomadic conquest left no havoc in its wake because the nomads did not destroy the irrigation systems and did not take up irrigated land for grazing.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_524_AfTochr]
♠ provision of irrigation works ♣ non-state ♥ Writing about the Khwarazm region, which fell outside of Yuezhi domination during this period, Mukhamedjanov says: 'Tolstov, in his study of the remains of the ancient irrigation works in Chorasmia, noted that it was precisely during the period of antiquity [4th century BC to 4th century AD according to the periodization used by the Karakalpak-Australian Archaeological Expedition][MAJIDBENAM_REF_525_AfTochr] that a school of irrigation engineers and high priests of science emerged at Chorasmia [...] The school included experts in mathematics, water engineering, cartography, astronomy and calendrical observations, which were of great importance for an extensive irrigation economy. The brunt of the task of building irrigation works was, however, borne by the peasants, and many irrigation systems were dug by labourers from the rural communities, without any particular expenditure of effort or contribution by the authorities'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_526_AfTochr] It seems likely that this was also the case in Yuezhi-controlled Bactria and southern Sogdiana, from which we have no evidence of a comparable school of engineering and science, or of any form of irrigation management by the state.
♠ Health infrastructure ♣ uncertain_present_absent ♥ Bernard thinks that a public fountain at the Graeco-Bactrian city of Aï Khanum 'probably also served as a bath for visitors to the adjacent gymnasium'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_527_AfTochr] However, Aï Khanum was sacked in nomadic invasions, and it is not clear whether the provision of public bathing facilities would have continued in other areas under the Yuezhi.

Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ ♥
♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥
♠ Theatres ♣ uncertain_present_absent ♥ Although the city of Ai Khanoum (which had a theatre)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_528_AfTochr] was sacked in nomadic invasions, it seems that Hellenistic urban culture in Bactria would have survived the arrival of the Yuezhi in other areas, including 'in and around the great cities of Bactra/Balkh and Qunduz'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_529_AfTochr] Perhaps this would have included theatres.
♠ Arenas ♣ ♥
♠ Ritual buildings/spaces ♣ present ♥ During the period of Yuezhi occupation, 'the renowned Sistan sanctuary of Kuh-i Khwaja also seem [sic] to have been rebuilt'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_530_AfTochr]
♠ Other ♣ ♥
♠ accessibility of entertainment buildings ♣ ♥
♠ Full-time entertainment specialists ♣ inferred present ♥ The main function of the theatre of Aï Khanum in the earlier Graeco-Bactrian period was 'to stage performances, either popular spectacles like pantomime and jugglers, or the Greek classical repertory'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_531_AfTochr] Aï Khanum was sacked in nomadic invasions, and it is not clear whether actors and performers of this sort would have been working under the Yuezhi. However, one thing that hints at the importance of performers in this period is the tiny golden lute or oud players recovered from Tomb II at Tillya Tepe. Schiltz comments that 'Music played an important role in Central Asia, particularly in the world of the nomads, a world of verbal history and heroic tales. These figures, more than simple musicians, could be reminiscent of the bards responsible for preserving the memory of the clan'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_532_AfTochr]
♠ status of full-time entertainment specialists ♣ ♥
♠ Access to education ♣ ♥ 'Like any Greek city of any significance, Aï Khanum had its gymnasium, devoted to intellectual and physical training'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_533_AfTochr] However, Aï Khanum was sacked in nomadic invasions, and it is unclear whether the Hellenistic institution of the gymnasium would have persisted in other areas of Bactria under the Yuezhi conquerors.

Biological Well-Being[edit]

Mortality
♠ Life expectancy ♣ unknown ♥ We do not have enough evidence to give a figure for this. 'In the time between the Greco-Bactrian period and the flowering of the Kushans, ancient records fail us'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_536_AfTochr]
♠ Infant mortality ♣ unknown ♥ We do not have enough evidence to give a figure for this. 'In the time between the Greco-Bactrian period and the flowering of the Kushans, ancient records fail us'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_537_AfTochr][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Disease
♠ Endemic disease ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Endemic malnutrition ♣ suspected unknown ♥Physiology
♠ Average height (male) ♣ unknown ♥ I have not found any figures for the average height of a man in this period and place, but Schiltz gives a rough height for the only man buried at Tillya Tepe: 1.7-1.85 m.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_538_AfTochr] However, Tillya Tepe is an incredibly rich site so cannot be taken as representative of the wider population.
♠ Average height (female) ♣ unknown ♥ I have not found any figures for the average height of a woman in this period and place, but Schiltz gives heights for two of the female burials at Tillya Tepe: 1.58 m and 1.52 m (so an average of 1.55 m).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_539_AfTochr] However, Tillya Tepe is an incredibly rich site so cannot be taken as representative of the wider population.

State Income[edit]

♠ Tribute ♣ present ♥ Narain states: 'It thus seems that the Ta-Yüeh-chih occupied only those parts of the Bactrian kingdom which lay north of the Oxus, but they had defeated the Ta-hsia without actually occupying their lands, and contented themselves for a time with the receipt of tribute'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_542_AfTochr] Writing more recently, Hill concurs: he believes the Yuezhi conquest of Bactria conformed to a 'common pattern of nomadic and semi-nomadic peoples when they conquered urban civilisations': the invaders would establish 'fortified seats of power' outside the cities and provide 'security while retaining some control over trade and communications, thus ensuring continuation of the all-important collection of taxes or tribute for the conquerors with minimum disruption'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_543_AfTochr] JR: The Chinese historian Sima Qian wrote that Kangju - identified by Benjamin as referring to the region of Sogdia - 'acknowledges nominal sovereignty to the Yuezhi people in the south and the Xiongnu in the east'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_544_AfTochr] The word 'nominal' in this English translation, and the fact that two different nomadic confederations, the Xiongnu and the Yuezhi, claimed dominance over Kangju, might indicate that Yuezhi administrative control over Sogdia would have been quite loose. Perhaps this was a tributary relationship?
♠ Staple ♣ inferred present ♥ Chinese historian Sima Qian records that before the Yuezhi were forced westwards by attacks from the Xiongnu, 'the authority of the Yüeh-chih ruler was accepted by the countries in Central Asia. They provided free food to envoys moving through their territories, but messengers from China had to pay for their food as well as their horses'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_545_AfTochr] This provisioning of Yuezhi envoys with food could count as a form of tribute. Perhaps once they settled close to the Oxus in the later 2nd century BCE, the Yuezhi would have continued to compel inhabitants of regions that accepted their authority to feed their officials. Enoki, Koshelenko and Haidary also speculate that the ceramic vessels found in nomad graves in Bactria were exacted as tribute from the farmers, but there is no concrete evidence for this - surely they could have been acquired through trade.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_546_AfTochr]
♠ Labor ♣ ♥
♠ Currency ♣ ♥Debt and Indemnities
♠ Polity has obligation to other polity or group ♣ ♥ JR: The Chinese historian Sima Qian wrote that Kangju - identified by Benjamin as referring to the region of Sogdia - 'acknowledges nominal sovereignty to the Yuezhi people in the south and the Xiongnu in the east'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_547_AfTochr] The word 'nominal' in this English translation, and the fact that two different nomadic confederations, the Xiongnu and the Yuezhi, claimed dominance over Kangju, might indicate that Yuezhi administrative control over Sogdia would have been quite loose. Perhaps this was a tributary relationship?
♠ Polity receives obligatory payment from other polity or group ♣ ♥ JR: The Chinese historian Sima Qian wrote that Kangju - identified by Benjamin as referring to the region of Sogdia - 'acknowledges nominal sovereignty to the Yuezhi people in the south and the Xiongnu in the east'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_548_AfTochr] The word 'nominal' in this English translation, and the fact that two different nomadic confederations, the Xiongnu and the Yuezhi, claimed dominance over Kangju, might indicate that Yuezhi administrative control over Sogdia would have been quite loose. Perhaps this was a tributary relationship?

Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ ♥
♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ unknown ♥ I have not found any figures for the staple cost of grain in this period. Discussing the nomadic states on the western fringes of China, Barfield says that 'Grain was one of the products most in demand by nomads, but because this trade was so ordinary it is scantily documented and scholars continue to argue over whether the steppe nomads included grain as a regular part of their diet'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_553_AfTochr] His comments seem likely to apply to the Yuezhi occupation of Transoxania and Bactria too.
♠ Top fortune ♣ unknown ♥ I have not found a numerical figure for the top fortune in this period. However, the six burials excavated at the site of Tillya Tepe in northern Afghanistan, dating to the mid-1st century CE, were 'extremely rich', containing 'goods from the length of Eurasia, including glass from the Roman west and mirrors from the Han Chinese east'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_554_AfTochr] There is some disagreement about the ethnic identity of these people, but they 'have generally been considered to be settled descendants of the nomadic Yuezhi and most scholars have described them as "proto-Kushans"'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_555_AfTochr]
♠ Other wealth distribution ♣ ♥ Barfield's model of nomadic states in Central Asia compares them to 'redistributive chiefdoms': they extract (or extort) tribute from sedentary populations and distribute the ensuing wealth among the 'tribes' that make up the nomadic confederation. 'Nomad states were more like redistributive chiefdoms. They lacked a strong class structure and the leaders' main responsibilities internally were organizational rather than extractive. They were expected to provide real benefits and prestige goods to component tribes and their leaders'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_556_AfTochr] He focuses on those nomadic 'empires' on the borders of the Chinese state, but perhaps this economic model could also apply to the Yuezhi in Transoxiana, who, if we believe the Chinese sources, migrated westward from their homeland in modern Gansu province.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Dedicated police force ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The Tocharians were a nomadic group who conquered a region (Transoxania and Bactria) which had settled peoples living in walled towns and cities. There were international markets here which apparently carried on as before. There is almost no data to draw from about the administrative structure of these towns at this time. One could infer the large successful markets had some form of policing to deter theft and fraud. The forms of policing of these urban regions may have been maintained through the Tocharian period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_557_AfTochr] We don't know whether they were specialists.
♠ Police levels ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The Tocharians were a nomadic group who conquered a region (Transoxania and Bactria) which had settled peoples living in walled towns and cities. There were international markets here which apparently carried on as before. There is almost no data to draw from about the administrative structure of these towns at this time. One could infer the large successful markets had some form of policing to deter theft and fraud. The forms of policing of these urban regions may have been maintained through the Tocharian period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_558_AfTochr]
♠ Proscriptions of elites and or property ♣ ♥ Use of the state to repress or purge rival elite groups.
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ ♥
♠ Military control over independent or semi-independent vassal states ♣ ♥
